## Transcript

- [00:00] Can week-old pizza cause psychedelic hallucinations?
- [00:02] Does coffee make you smarter?
- [00:04] Or does it just make you do dumb stuff faster?
- [00:06] Like a bunch of psychology itself, questions like this can seem pretty intuitive.
- [00:09] I mean, people may not be the easiest organisms to understand, but you're a person, right?
- [00:13] So you must be qualified to draw, like, some conclusions about other people and what makes
- [00:18] them tick.
- [00:19] But it's important to realize that your intuition isn't always right.
- [00:22] In fact, sometimes it is exactly wrong, and we tend to grossly underestimate the dangers
- [00:27] of false intuition.
- [00:28] If you have an idea about a person and their behavior that turns out to be right, that
- [00:32] reinforces your trust in your intuition.
- [00:34] Like if one of my buddies, Bob, begins eating that deep-dish pizza that's been in the fridge
- [00:38] for the past week but he eats it anyway and soon starts to wig out, I'll say "Dude, I
- [00:42] told you so".
- [00:43] But if I'm wrong and he's totally fine, I probably won't even think about it ever again.
- [00:49] This is known as 'Hindsight Bias" or the "I-Knew-It-All-Along" phenomenon.
- [00:52] This doesn't mean the common sense is wrong, it just means that our intuitive sense more
- [00:56] easily describes what just happened, than what will happen in the future.
- [01:00] Another reason you can't blindly trust your intuition is your natural tendency toward
- [01:04] overconfidence.
- [01:05] Sometimes, you just really, really feel like you're right about people when actually you're
- [01:11] really, really wrong.
- [01:12] We've all been there.
- [01:13] We also tend to perceive order in random events, which can lead to false assumptions.
- [01:18] For example, if you flip a coin five times you have equal chances of getting all tails
- [01:22] as you do getting alternating heads and tails.
- [01:25] But we see the series of five tails as something unusual, as a streak, and thus giving that
- [01:30] result some kind of meaning that it very definitely does not have.
- [01:33] That is why we have the methods and safe-guards of psychological research and experimentation,
- [01:39] and the glorious process of scientific inquiry.
- [01:42] They help us to get around these problems and basically save the study of our minds
- [01:46] from the stupidity of our minds.
- [01:48] So I hope that it won't be a spoiler if I tell you now that pizza won't make you trip,
- [01:53] and coffee doesn't make you smart.
- [01:59] Sorry.
- [02:01] [Intro]
- [02:04] In most ways psychological research is no different than any other scientific discipline,
- [02:10] like step one is always figuring out how to ask general questions about your subject and
- [02:15] turn them into measurable, testable propositions.
- [02:18] This is called operationalizing your questions.
- [02:21] So you know how the scientific method works -- it starts with a question and a theory,
- [02:25] and I don't mean theory in the sense of like, a hunch that say, a quad-shot of espresso
- [02:29] makes you think better.
- [02:31] Instead, in science a theory is what explains and organizes lots of different observations
- [02:36] and predicts outcomes.
- [02:37] And when you come up with a testable prediction, that's your hypothesis.
- [02:41] Once your theory and hypothesis are in place, you need a clear and common language to report
- [02:45] them with, so for example, defining exactly what you mean by "thinking better" with your
- [02:49] espresso hypothesis will allow other researchers to replicate the experiment.
- [02:53] And replication is key.
- [02:55] You can watch a person exhibit a certain behavior once, and it won't prove very much, but if
- [03:00] you keep getting consistent results, even as you change subjects or situations, you're
- [03:04] probably on to something.
- [03:05] This is a problem with one popular type of psychological research: case studies, which
- [03:10] take an in-depth look at one individual.
- [03:13] Case studies can sometimes be misleading, because by their nature, they can't be replicated,
- [03:17] so they run the risk of over-generalizing.
- [03:18] Still, they're good at showing us what CAN happen, and end up framing questions for more
- [03:23] extensive and generalizable studies.
- [03:25] They're also often memorable and a great story telling device psychologists use to observe
- [03:29] and describe behavior.
- [03:30] Like, say the smell of coffee makes Carl suddenly anxious and irritable -- that obviously doesn't
- [03:36] mean that it has that same effect on everyone.
- [03:37] In fact, Carl has terrible memories associated with that smell, and so his case is actually
- [03:42] quite rare.
- [03:44] Poor Carl.
- [03:45] But you would still have to look at lots of other cases to determine that conclusively.
- [03:48] Another popular method of psychological research is naturalistic observation, where researchers
- [03:53] simply watch behavior in a natural environment, whether that's chimps poking ant-hills in
- [03:57] the jungle, kids clowning in a classroom or drunk dudes yelling at soccer games.
- [04:01] The idea is to let the subjects just do their thing without trying to manipulate or control
- [04:06] the situation.
- [04:07] So yeah, basically just spying on people.
- [04:09] Like case studies, naturalistic observations are great at describing behavior, but they're
- [04:13] very limited in explaining it.
- [04:15] Psychologists can also collect behavioral data using surveys or interviews, asking people
- [04:19] to report their opinions and behaviors.
- [04:22] Sexuality researcher Alfred Kinsey famously used this technique when he surveyed thousands
- [04:26] of men and women on their sexual history and published his findings in a pair of revolutionary
- [04:31] texts, Sexual Behavior in the Human Male and Female respectively.
- [04:35] Surveys are a great way to access consciously held attitudes and beliefs, but how to ask
- [04:40] the questions can be tricky; subtle word choices can influence results.
- [04:44] For example more forceful words like "ban" or "censor" may elicit different reactions
- [04:48] than "limit" or "not allow".
- [04:50] Asking "Do you believe in space aliens?" is a much different question than "Do you think
- [04:54] that there is intelligent life somewhere else in the universe?"
- [04:56] It's the same question, but in the first the subject might assume you mean aliens visiting
- [05:00] earth, and making crop circles and abducting people and poking them.
- [05:04] And if how you phrase surveys is important, so is who you ask.
- [05:06] I could ask a room full of students at a pacifist club meeting what they think about arms control,
- [05:11] but the result wouldn't be a representative measure of where students stand, because there's
- [05:15] a pretty clear sampling bias at work here.
- [05:17] To fairly represent a population, I'd need to get a random sample where all members of
- [05:21] the target group, in this case students, had an equal chance of being selected to answer
- [05:26] the question.
- [05:27] So once you've described behavior with surveys, case studies, or naturalistic observation,
- [05:31] you can start making sense out of it, and even predict future behavior.
- [05:35] One way to do that is to look at one trait or behavior is related to another, or how
- [05:39] they correlate.
- [05:40] So let's get back to my buddy Bob who seems to think that his refrigerator is actually
- [05:43] some kind of time machine that can preserve food indefinitely.
- [05:46] Let's say that Bob has just tucked into a lunch of questionable leftovers, pizza that
- [05:50] may very well have had a little bit of fungus on it.
- [05:52] But he was hungry, and lazy, and so he doused it in Sriracha.
- [05:56] Suddenly, he starts seeing things: green armadillos with laser beam eyes.
- [05:59] From here we could deduce that eating unknown fungus predicts hallucination, that's a correlation.
- [06:04] But correlation is not causation.
- [06:07] Yes, it makes sense that eating questionable fungus would cause hallucinations, but it's
- [06:11] possible that Bob was already on the verge of a psychotic episode, and those fuzzy leftovers
- [06:16] were actually benign.
- [06:17] Or there could be an entirely different factor involved, like maybe he hadn't slept in 72
- [06:22] hours, or had an intense migraine coming on, and one of those factors caused his hallucinations.
- [06:26] It's tempting to draw conclusions from correlations, but it's super-important to remember that
- [06:30] correlations predict the possibility of cause-and-effect relationships; they cannot prove them.
- [06:36] So we've talked about how to describe behavior without manipulating it and how to make connections
- [06:40] and predictions from those findings.
- [06:42] But that can only take you so far; to really get to the bottom of cause-and-effect behaviors,
- [06:47] you're gonna have to start experimenting.
- [06:49] Experiments allow investigators to isolate different effects by manipulating an independent
- [06:53] variable, and keeping all other variables constant, or as constant as you can.
- [06:58] This means that they need at least two groups: the experimental group, which is gonna get
- [07:01] messed with, and the control group, which is not gonna get messed with.
- [07:05] Just as surveys use random samples, experimental researchers need to randomly assign participants
- [07:09] to each group to minimize potential confounding variables, or outside factors that may skew
- [07:14] the results.
- [07:15] You don't want all grumpy teenagers in one group and all wealthy Japanese surfers in
- [07:18] the other; they gotta mingle.
- [07:19] Now sometimes one or both groups are not informed about what's actually being tested.
- [07:24] For example, researchers can test how substances effect people by comparing their effects to
- [07:29] placebos, or inert substances.
- [07:31] And often, the researchers themselves don't know which group is experimental and which
- [07:35] is control, so they don't unintentionally influence the results through their own behavior,
- [07:39] in which case it's called, you guessed it, a double blind procedure.
- [07:44] So let's put these ideas into practice in our own little experiment.
- [07:47] Like all good work, it starts with a question.
- [07:49] So the other day my friend Bernice and I were debating.
- [07:51] We were debating caffeine's effect on the brain.
- [07:53] Personally, she convinced that coffee helps her focus and think better, but I get all
- [07:58] jittery like a caged meerkat and can't focus on anything.
- [08:00] And because we know that overconfidence can lead you to believe things that are not true,
- [08:04] we decided to use some critical thinking.
- [08:06] So let's figure out our question: "Do humans solve problems faster when given caffeine?"
- [08:10] Now we gotta boil that down into a testable prediction.
- [08:13] Remember: keep it clear, simple, and eloquent so that it can be replicated.
- [08:17] "Caffeine makes me smarter" is not a great hypothesis.
- [08:20] A better one would be, say, "Adult humans given caffeine will navigate a maze faster
- [08:25] than humans not given caffeine."
- [08:27] The caffeine dosage is your independent variable, the thing that you can change.
- [08:31] So, you'll need some coffee.
- [08:32] Your result or dependent variable, the thing that depends on the thing that you can change
- [08:37] is going to be the speed at which the subject navigates through this giant corn maze.
- [08:40] Go out on the street, wrangle up a bunch of different kinds of people and randomly assign
- [08:44] them into three different groups.
- [08:46] Also at this point, the American Psychological Association suggests that you acquire everyone's
- [08:49] informed consent to participate.
- [08:51] You don't want to force anyone to be in your experiment, no matter how cool you think it
- [08:55] is.
- [08:56] So the control group gets a placebo, in this case decaf.
- [08:58] Experimental group one gets a low dose of caffeine, which we'll define at a 100 milligrams;
- [09:03] just an eye opener, like, a cup of coffee's worth.
- [09:05] Experimental group two gets 500 milligrams, more than a quad shot of espresso dunked in
- [09:10] a Red Bull.
- [09:11] Once you dose everyone, turn them lose in the maze and wait at the other end with a
- [09:14] stopwatch.
- [09:15] All that's left is to measure your results from the three different groups and compare
- [09:18] them to see if there were any conclusive results.
- [09:20] If the highly dosed folks got through it twice as fast as the low dose and the placebo groups,
- [09:24] then Bernice's hypothesis was correct, and she can rub my face in it saying she was right
- [09:28] all along, but really that would just be the warm flush of hindsight bias telling her something
- [09:33] she didn't really know until we tested it.
- [09:36] Then, because we've used clear language and defined our parameters, other curious minds
- [09:40] can easily replicate this experiment, and we can eventually pool all the data together
- [09:44] and have something solid to say about what that macchiato was doing to your cognition
- [09:48] or at least the speed at which you can run through a maze.
- [09:51] Science: probably the best tool that you have for understanding other people.
- [09:54] Thanks for watching this episode of Crash Course Psychology; if you paid attention you
- [09:58] learned how to apply the scientific method to psychological research through case studies,
- [10:02] naturalistic observation, surveys, and interviews and experimentation.
- [10:06] You also learned about different kinds of bias in experimentation and how research practices
- [10:11] help us avoid them.
